                      1. The Transformations of China in the 1970s and Hong Kong

                      (Carroll 2007: 176‚Äì179; Lui Tai-lok 2020: 150‚Äì154)

                      As Hong Kong entered the 1970s, the shocks of two prior riots forced the colonial government to accelerate its pace of reform. The 1971 population census revealed a demographic continuation of the trend noted in 1966, with over half of the population being locally born, ushering in a new era for the decade. The locally born younger generation abandoned the refugee mentality of their predecessors. While they developed a stronger sense of identification with and expectations for Hong Kong society, they could not fully escape the profound changes taking place in China during the 1970s. A distinct local society was gradually forming, yet it remained inseparable from its connection with China.

                      Undoubtedly, changes in Sino-U.S. relations were a crucial factor behind the dramatic transformations in China during the 1970s. In 1971, China invited the U.S. table tennis team for a visit, an act widely interpreted as an attempt to break the ice in Sino-U.S. relations through ‚Äúping-pong diplomacy.‚Äù In July, U.S. Secretary of State Henry Kissinger made a secret visit to Beijing, during which he pledged U.S. support for the People‚Äôs Republic of China to gain membership in the United Nations and a seat on the Security Council during the upcoming General Assembly session. Kissinger also agreed on a visit by President Richard Nixon to China before May 1972.

                      After Kissinger‚Äôs visit, the United Nations General Assembly passed Resolution 2758 on October 25, which restored the People‚Äôs Republic of China‚Äôs legitimate rights within the United Nations. The following day, Chiang Kai-shek issued a statement announcing the Republic of China‚Äôs withdrawal from the United Nations. This vote was considered a significant diplomatic victory for the People‚Äôs Republic of China, with U.S. support playing a pivotal role.

                      In February 1972, at the invitation of Premier Zhou Enlai, President Nixon visited China, becoming the first U.S. president to do so in history. Following the visit, China and the U.S. issued the Shanghai Communiqu√©, marking a gradual normalization of relations after over two decades of hostility.

                      However, the changes in Sino-U.S. relations were part of a broader triangular contest among China, the U.S., and the Soviet Union. For China, which was still engulfed in the fervor of the Cultural Revolution, these developments seemed to bring little immediate change. It wasn‚Äôt until Mao Zedong‚Äôs declining health in 1976 that these shifts could be seen as precursors to major transformations. The subsequent arrests of the Gang of Four, Deng Xiaoping‚Äôs return to power, and China‚Äôs emergence from the shadows of the Cultural Revolution in 1979 marked the nation‚Äôs formal farewell to revolutionary politics and the beginning of a new era of economic reform and opening up. That same year, China and the U.S. formally established diplomatic relations, with Deng Xiaoping visiting the U.S. shortly afterward.

                      What impact did the transformations in China during the 1970s have on Hong Kong?

                      Firstly, following China‚Äôs entry into the United Nations, its UN representative Huang Hua declared in March 1972 that Hong Kong‚Äôs political status was ‚ÄúChinese territory occupied by British authorities‚Äù and demanded the removal of Hong Kong and Macau from the UN list of colonies. Huang emphasized that the Hong Kong issue fell within China‚Äôs sovereignty and that the Chinese government would resolve it ‚Äúat an appropriate time, in an appropriate manner.‚Äù On November 2, 1972, the United Nations General Assembly passed Resolution 2908 with 99 votes in favor and 5 against, officially removing Hong Kong and Macau from the list of colonies. The United Kingdom did not object to this resolution.

                      The most significant implication of this move was the deprivation of the right to self-determination, previously granted under the UN‚Äôs 1960 Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples.

                      In response to these changes and to align with the demands of both the British and Chinese governments, Hong Kong‚Äôs status shifted from a Crown Colony to a Dependent Territory. However, the term ‚Äúcolony‚Äù remained on its banknotes until 1985. Furthermore, the title of Colonial Secretary was changed to Chief Secretary, and the United Kingdom ceased its annual reports on Hong Kong to the UN Secretary-General.

                      What did Huang Hua mean by resolving the Hong Kong issue ‚Äúat an appropriate time, in an appropriate manner‚Äù? At the time, no one could provide a definitive answer. However, when Portugal proposed returning Macau in 1974, and the Chinese government declined, it sparked speculation that China might also be reluctant to reclaim Hong Kong.

                      Nonetheless, with the lease on the New Territories set to expire in 1997, the Hong Kong government could no longer issue land leases extending beyond that date. The year 1997 undoubtedly became a ‚Äúdeadline‚Äù for Hong Kong‚Äôs future. Murray MacLehose, who became Governor of Hong Kong in November 1971, was aware of this issue even before assuming office. He summarized London‚Äôs stance on Hong Kong as follows:

  1. avoid direct confrontation with the Chinese government and refrain from initiating negotiations on Hong Kong‚Äôs future in the short term;
  2. design policies that could sustain public confidence in the government, thereby buying sufficient time to wait for favorable conditions for negotiations with China. He emphasized, ‚ÄúThe critical moment will not begin until the late 1970s, but the planning process must start now.‚Äù

    In January 1973, MacLehose stated in his 1972 annual report that ‚Äúsooner or later, we must discuss with the Chinese government what will happen when the lease on the New Territories expires in 1997.‚Äù He firmly believed that before entering negotiations, the Hong Kong government needed to ‚Äúput its house in order,‚Äù meaning to develop Hong Kong to be prosperous, cohesive, and content, so that it would not become a target of criticism. He also stressed that if the Chinese government considered Hong Kong‚Äôs economic benefits, ‚Äúit might be unwilling to reclaim Hong Kong‚Äù and might even consider granting Hong Kong special status under its sovereignty. In sum, Hong Kong needed to become ‚Äúa model city.‚Äù However, MacLehose cautioned that this should be pursued discreetly, with minimal rhetoric and without overtly challenging China.

    In May 1974, Edward Heath, former British Prime Minister and leader of the Conservative Party, visited Beijing. During a banquet hosted by Vice Premier Deng Xiaoping, Deng stated that the Hong Kong issue would be resolved at an appropriate time. In March 1979, the Chinese government invited MacLehose to visit Beijing, marking the first official visit by a sitting Hong Kong Governor to China. MacLehose viewed this as an opportunity to discuss the lease of the New Territories. During his meeting with Deng Xiaoping, MacLehose proposed that the Legislative Council could unilaterally disregard the 1997 deadline without asserting sovereignty over New Territories land. This suggestion was rejected by Deng. It is widely believed that during this meeting, Deng expressed China‚Äôs intention to reclaim Hong Kong by 1997. However, upon returning to Hong Kong, MacLehose only stated that Deng was committed to resolving the Hong Kong issue eventually and reassured Hong Kong investors to ‚Äúrest assured.‚Äù

    In 1981, the United Kingdom passed the British Nationality Act, which downgraded the status of 2.5 million Hong Kong residents eligible for British passports to British Dependent Territories Citizens (BDTC), effectively stripping them of the right to reside in the United Kingdom. This move reflected Britain‚Äôs preparations for 1997 and closed the door on Hong Kong residents migrating to Britain.

    1974Âπ¥5ÊúàÔºåÂâõËæ≠‰ªªÈ¶ñÁõ∏ÁöÑËã±Âúã‰øùÂÆàÈª®Èª®È≠ÅÂ∏åÊÄùÔºàEdward Richard George HeathÔºâË®™ÂïèÂåó‰∫¨ÔºåÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥ÂâØÁ∏ΩÁêÜÂú®Ê≠°ËøéÂ∏åÊÄùÁöÑÂÆ¥ÊúÉ‰∏äË°®Á§∫ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂïèÈ°åÂ∞áÂú®ÈÅ©Áï∂ÊôÇÂÄôËß£Ê±∫„ÄÇ1979Âπ¥3ÊúàÔºå‰∏≠ÂúãÊîøÂ∫úÈÇÄË´ãÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Ë®™ÂïèÂåó‰∫¨ÔºåÊòØÈ¶ñÊ¨°Âú®‰ªªÊ∏ØÁù£ÂÆòÂºèË®™Âïè‰∏≠ÂúãÔºåÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Ë¶ñÁÇ∫Ë®éË´ñÊñ∞ÁïåÁßüÁ¥ÑÂïèÈ°åÁöÑÊ©üÊúÉ„ÄÇ‰ªñÂú®Âåó‰∫¨‰∏≠ÂúãÊñ∞È†òÂ∞é‰∫∫ÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥ÊúÉÈù¢ÊôÇÔºåÂª∫Ë≠∞Á´ãÊ≥ïÂ±ÄÂú®‰∏çÊèêÂá∫ÊìÅÊúâÊñ∞ÁïåÂúüÂú∞‰∏ªÊ¨äÁöÑÊÉÖÊ≥Å‰∏ãÔºåÂèØ‰ª•ÂñÆÊñπÈù¢‰∏çÁêÜÊúÉ1997Âπ¥ÁöÑÊúüÈôê„ÄÇÊ≠§Âª∫Ë≠∞Ë¢´ÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥ÊãíÁµï„ÄÇ‰∏ÄËà¨Áõ∏‰ø°ÔºåÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥Âú®Ê≠§Ê¨°ÊúÉË´á‰∏≠Ë°®Á§∫Â∞áÊñº1997Âπ¥Êî∂ÂõûÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ‰ΩÜÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©ÂõûÂà∞È¶ôÊ∏ØÊôÇÔºåÂè™Ë°®Á§∫ÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥‰∏ªÂºµ‰∏≠ÂúãÊúÄÁµÇÊúÉËß£Ê±∫È¶ôÊ∏ØÂïèÈ°åÔºå‰∏¶Ë°®Á§∫È¶ôÊ∏ØÊäïË≥áËÄÖÂèØ‰ª•„ÄéÊîæÂøÉ„Äè„Äç„ÄÇ

    1981Âπ¥ÔºåËã±ÂúãÈÄöÈÅéÁöÑ„ÄäËã±ÂúãÂúãÁ±çÊ≥ï„ÄãÔºåÊää250Ëê¨ÂéüÊú¨ÊìÅÊúâË≥áÊ†ºÂèñÂæóËã±ÂúãË≠∑ÁÖßÁöÑÈ¶ôÊ∏ØËèØ‰∫∫ÁöÑË∫´ÂàÜÈôçÁÇ∫„ÄåËã±ÂúãÂ±¨ÂúüÂÖ¨Ê∞ë„ÄçÔºàBritish Dependent Territories Citizens, BDTCÔºâÔºåÂç≥ÂâùÂ•™‰∫ÜÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫∫ÂèñÂæóËã±ÂúãÁöÑÂ±ÖÁïôÊ¨ä„ÄÇÊ≠§ËàâÂèçÊò†Ëã±ÂúãÂ∑≤ÁÇ∫1997Âπ¥‰ΩúÂ•ΩÈ†êÂÇôÔºå‰∏¶Èóú‰∏äÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫∫ÁßªÂ±ÖËã±ÂúãÁöÑÂæåÈñÄ„ÄÇ

    (2) The Transformation of Hong Kong in the 1970s

    (Carroll 2007: 168‚Äì176; Lui Tai-lok 2020: 141‚Äì186; Yep 2024)

    The 1970s are often referred to as the ‚ÄúMacLehose Era.‚Äù Sir Murray MacLehose served as the Governor of Hong Kong from November 1971 until May 1982, with his tenure extended four times between 1977 and 1979, making him the longest-serving governor in Hong Kong‚Äôs colonial history. MacLehose‚Äôs policies and reforms were, on the one hand, a continuation of the principles laid out by his predecessor, David Trench, which sought to address the riots of 1966 and 1967 through reform. On the other hand, they were intended to address the question of Hong Kong‚Äôs future, aiming to transform Hong Kong into a ‚Äúmodel city‚Äù that would secure favorable terms in future Sino-British negotiations.

    During MacLehose‚Äôs tenure, significant advancements were made in various areas: public housing (the Ten-Year Housing Program in 1973 and the Home Ownership Scheme in 1976), nine years of free compulsory education (1978), public assistance (allowances for the disabled and elderly in 1973 and the Social Security Green Paper in 1977), transportation (the proposal to build the Mass Transit Railway in 1972), labor legislation (the Severance Payment Ordinance in 1974, amendments to the Labour Tribunal Ordinance in 1974, and seven days of paid annual leave in 1977), and social welfare (the White Papers on Social Welfare in 1973 and 1979).

    In governance, all ex officio members of the Urban Council were removed in 1973, leaving only appointed and elected members. Although no significant administrative or legislative council reforms occurred, the number of Legislative Council seats was increased in 1976, and several grassroots-oriented unofficial members were appointed, including Reverend Joyce Bennett (1923‚Äì2015) and Father Patrick Terence McGovern (1920‚Äì1984).

    Other initiatives included the Clean Hong Kong Campaign (1972), the Fight Violent Crime Campaign (1973), the Country Parks Program (1976), and the legalization of Chinese as an official language (1974). A major achievement was the establishment of the Independent Commission Against Corruption (ICAC) in 1974 to combat entrenched corruption.

    The extensive reforms led to a substantial increase in government expenditure, which rose by 50% between 1970 and 1972 and continued to grow thereafter. The 1975 budget was the largest postwar budget in Hong Kong‚Äôs history, with a deficit of 900 million Hong Kong dollars and the introduction of 26 new taxes across four categories. There is no doubt that the MacLehose era marked a departure from laissez-faire economic policies toward the establishment of a welfare state.

    Overall, the MacLehose era was a period of robust economic growth in Hong Kong, with a consistent rise in GDP. Hong Kong became a global leader in the export value and volume of garments, toys, plastics, and watches, establishing itself as one of Asia‚Äôs leading light industrial hubs. The government‚Äôs implementation of the Home Ownership Scheme also led to a surge in private residential construction, which further spurred the real estate sector and contributed to economic growth.

    However, the period also saw challenges. The 1973 oil crisis triggered by the Middle East war caused a stock market crash in July, with the Hang Seng Index plunging by 91.54% (from a high of 1,174.96 in March 1973 to 150.11 on December 10, 1974). Between 1972 and 1975, Hong Kong experienced an economic downturn. Trade protectionist measures by various countries caused a 7% decline in real export volumes in 1974, leading to factory closures and widespread underemployment. By 1975, unemployment had risen to 200,000, with 120,000 in the industrial sector and 80,000 in commerce.

    By 1976, the economy began to recover, and in 1979, China‚Äôs economic reforms provided new opportunities for growth in Hong Kong.

    Êï¥È´îËÄåË®ÄÔºåÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©ÊôÇ‰ª£ÊòØÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ∂ìÊøüËì¨ÂãÉÂ¢ûÈï∑ÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊú¨Âú∞ÁîüÁî¢Á∏ΩÂÄºÊåÅÁ∫åÂ¢ûÂä†„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂú®ÊàêË°£„ÄÅÁé©ÂÖ∑„ÄÅÂ°ëËÜ†ÂèäÈêòÈå∂Á≠âÁî¢ÂìÅÁöÑÂá∫Âè£ÈáëÈ°çÂèäÂá∫Âè£Êï∏ÈáèÔºåÂ∑≤ÂêçÂàó‰∏ñÁïåÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÔºåÊàêÁÇ∫‰∫ûÊ¥≤ËºïÂ∑•Ê•≠ÂìÅË£ΩÈÄ†‰∏≠ÂøÉ‰πã‰∏Ä„ÄÇÂæå‰æÜÊîøÂ∫úÂØ¶ÊñΩ„ÄåÂ±ÖËÄÖÊúâÂÖ∂Â±ã„ÄçË®àÂäÉÔºå‰πü‰øÉ‰ΩøÁßÅ‰∫∫‰ΩèÂÆÖÊ®ìÂÆáÈáèÁöÑÂ¢ûÂä†ÔºåÊàøÂú∞Áî¢Ê•≠‰πüÊé®ÂãïÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ∂ìÊøüÊàêÈï∑„ÄÇ‰∏çÈÅéÔºåÊúüÈñì‰πüÂõ†‰∏≠Êù±Êà∞Áà≠ÂºïÁôºÁü≥Ê≤πÂç±Ê©üÔºåÁµêÊûúÈ¶ôÊ∏ØËÇ°Â∏ÇÂú®1973Âπ¥7ÊúàÁôºÁîüËÇ°ÁÅΩÔºåÊÅÜÁîüÊåáÊï∏Á¥ØË®à‰ΩéÂπÖÈÅî91.54%Ôºà1973Âπ¥3ÊúàÊúÄÈ´ò‰Ωç1174.96ÈªûÔºåËá≥1974Âπ¥12Êúà10Êó•150.11ÈªûÔºâ„ÄÇ1972Ëá≥75Âπ¥ÈñìÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ∂ìÊøüÂá∫ÁèæËï≠Ê¢ùÂ±ÄÈù¢„ÄÇÁî±ÊñºÂêÑÂúãÂØ¶ÊñΩË≤øÊòì‰øùË≠∑‰∏ªÁæ©Ôºå1974Âπ¥ÂØ¶Ë≥™Âá∫Âè£Èáè‰∏ãÈôç7%ÔºåÂ∞éËá¥Â∑•Âª†ÈñãÂ∑•‰∏çË∂≥„ÄÇ1975Âπ¥ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ§±Ê•≠‰∫∫Êï∏È´òÈÅî20Ëê¨ÔºàÂ∑•Ê•≠12Ëê¨‰∫∫ÔºåÂïÜÊ•≠8Ëê¨‰∫∫Ôºâ„ÄÇ1976Âπ¥ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ∂ìÊøüÈñãÂßãËµ∞Âá∫Âõ∞Â±ÄÔºåÂà∞1979Âπ¥‰∏≠ÂúãÊé®Ë°åÁ∂ìÊøüÊîπÈù©ÔºåÂèàÁÇ∫È¶ôÊ∏ØÊèê‰æõ‰∫ÜÁ∂ìÊøüÂ¢ûÈï∑ÁöÑÊñ∞ÂÖÉÁ¥†„ÄÇ

    1. Nationalism and Hong Kong Identity

    (Lui Tai-lok 2020: 188‚Äì207; Law Wing-sang 2015; Law Wing-sang 2017: 76‚Äì80)

    It is widely believed that after the two riots in the 1960s, the colonial government deliberately fostered and strengthened a sense of belonging among Hong Kong citizens. Coupled with the Cultural Revolution and the 1967 riots, which led to resistance against Communist China, the sense of a ‚ÄúHong Konger‚Äù identity began to gradually take shape.

    However, in the early 1970s, there was still a strong sense of curiosity among young university students in Hong Kong to learn about their ‚Äúmotherland.‚Äù Specifically, this was reflected in the Chinese Language Movement (1968‚Äì1971) and the Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement (1970‚Äì1972). Both were student-led social movements with strong nationalist orientations.

    The Chinese Language Movement aimed to make Chinese an official language in Hong Kong, challenging the colonial policy of long-standing official preference for English. Advocates argued that most Hong Kong residents spoke Chinese as their mother tongue and sought equal treatment for the language. By 1970, various academic groups had mobilized to gain broader social attention for the movement. Some voices even suggested that the movement should go beyond nationalism to incorporate anti-colonialism. In September 1970, the government began studying the use of Chinese in official documents. In November, the Legislative Council amended its rules to allow council members to speak in English or Cantonese. In October 1971, simultaneous interpretation between English and Cantonese was introduced for the first time. Finally, in February 1974, the Legislative Council passed a third reading, formally designating Chinese as an official language and allowing Chinese dialects to be used in courts.

    The Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement arose in response to the U.S. decision to transfer the Diaoyu Islands and its affiliated islets to Japan. This incited protests from Hong Kong university students. In July 1971, the Hong Kong Federation of Students held a demonstration in Victoria Park. Riot police were deployed, resulting in clashes and arrests. In August and September, the police permitted further demonstrations in Victoria Park, which attracted nearly a thousand participants. The Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement not only heightened the nationalist sentiment among Hong Kong students but also evolved into a patriotic movement aligned with Communist China as students sought a sense of belonging through nationalist fervor. This identification with ‚Äúthe motherland‚Äù reflected the inability of young students, under colonial circumstances, to fully integrate into the existing system. Instead, they sought identity through nationalism and patriotic sentiment.

    Both the Chinese Language Movement and the Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement exemplified the fervent atmosphere of student activism in Hong Kong during the ‚Äúfiery years‚Äù of the 1970s. In December 1971, the University of Hong Kong Students‚Äô Union organized the first ‚ÄúHomeland Tour,‚Äù which was warmly received by mainland officials. This ignited a wave of interest among university students to organize study tours to China. During this period, campus ideology leaned toward leftist socialism, with the ‚ÄúCultural Purists‚Äù dominating the student movement. Student unions organized ‚ÄúChina Weeks,‚Äù which focused on showcasing the achievements of the Communist Party, praising Mao Zedong, and celebrating the Cultural Revolution. China‚Äôs rising international status‚Äîgaining a seat in the United Nations and the global ‚ÄúChina fever‚Äù in the West‚Äîfurther reinforced the beliefs of the Cultural Purists. In March 1975, they introduced the slogan: ‚ÄúLook at the world, understand the motherland, care about society, and fight for student rights,‚Äù reaffirming Hong Kong‚Äôs inseparability from China.

    In contrast to the Cultural Purists, there was another faction within university campuses known as the Social Activists. While also aligned with leftist ideologies, the Social Activists believed that the student movement should address the issues facing Hong Kong society and pursue social reform through ‚Äúanti-capitalism and anti-colonialism.‚Äù They rejected the unconditional alignment with Communist China advocated by the Cultural Purists, placing greater emphasis on social engagement rather than emotional nationalism as a means to understand China.

    After 1976, with the death of Mao Zedong, the end of the Cultural Revolution, and the downfall of the Gang of Four, the Cultural Purists gradually declined. Consequently, the Hong Kong student movement shifted toward the Social Activists‚Äô approach, focusing on colonial social issues. By the mid-1970s, the general public in Hong Kong had also become more concerned with various social problems, leading to the emergence of social struggles centered on protecting rights and interests.

    Simultaneously, the reforms during the MacLehose era addressed some of the public‚Äôs expectations of the colonial government. Hong Kong society began to develop a localized political agenda, forming a ‚Äúself-contained‚Äù local society that moved beyond the ideological struggles between the Chinese Nationalists and Communists or the broader left-right divide.

    By 1978, Hong Kong‚Äôs population had reached 4.5 million. However, it also faced a surge of illegal immigrants from China. As early as November 1974, the Hong Kong government had reached a verbal agreement with Guangdong authorities allowing the repatriation of captured Chinese illegal immigrants to the mainland (though immigrants who successfully entered urban areas would be permitted to stay). In 1979, Xi Zhongxun, Director of the Guangdong Revolutionary Committee, stated that the Chinese government was taking ‚Äúeffective measures‚Äù to address the ‚Äúabnormal situation‚Äù of legal and illegal immigration from China to Hong Kong.

    Faced with the influx of ‚Äúnew immigrants‚Äù from China, Hong Kong residents developed a ‚Äúself-protective‚Äù mindset, consciously defining who belonged and who did not. Concerns arose over whether the ‚Äúnew immigrants‚Äù would place a burden on Hong Kong and impact the rights of local residents.

    ‰øùÈá£ÈÅãÂãïÁöÑÂá∫ÁèæÔºå‰πÉÈáùÂ∞çÁæéÂúãÂ∞áÈá£È≠öÂè∞ÂèäÂÖ∂ÈôÑÂ±¨Â≥∂Â∂ºÁßª‰∫§Êó•Êú¨ÔºåÊøÄÁôºÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ∞à‰∏äÂ≠∏ÁîüÁöÑÊäóË≠∞Ë°åÂãï„ÄÇ1971Âπ¥7ÊúàÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ∞à‰∏äÂ≠∏ÁîüËÅØÊúÉÂú®Á∂≠Â§öÂà©‰∫ûÂÖ¨ÂúíËàâË°å„Äå‰øùÈá£„ÄçÁ§∫Â®ÅÔºåË≠¶ÊñπÂá∫ÂãïÈò≤Êö¥ÈöäÊØÜÊâì‰∏¶ÊãòÊçïÂ≠∏ÁîüÔºåÈÄ†ÊàêÊµÅË°Ä‰∫ã‰ª∂„ÄÇ8 ÊúàÂèä9ÊúàÔºåË≠¶ÊñπÊâπÂáÜÂú®Á∂≠ÂúíËàâË°åÁöÑ‰øùÈá£Á§∫Â®ÅÔºåÊúâËøëÂçÉ‰∫∫ÂèÉËàá„ÄÇ‰øùÈá£ÈÅãÂãï‰∏çÂÉÖÊèêÂçáÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ≠∏ÁîüÁöÑÊ∞ëÊóèÊÑüÊÉÖÔºåÊõ¥Âõ†Ê∞ëÊóè‰∏ªÁæ©ÊÑüÊÉÖÈúÄË¶ÅÂ∞ãÊâæÊ≠∏Â±¨ÔºåÂõ†ËÄåÊºîËÆäÊàê„ÄåË™çÂêåÔºàÂÖ±Áî¢Ôºâ‰∏≠Âúã„ÄçÁöÑÊÑõÂúãÈÅãÂãï„ÄÇÈÄôÁ®ÆÂ∞ç„ÄåÁ•ñÂúã„ÄçÁöÑË™çÂêåÔºåÂèçÊò†Âá∫Âú®ÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞ËôïÂ¢É‰∏≠ÔºåÂπ¥ÈùíÂ≠∏ÁîüÁÑ°Ê≥ïÊäïÂÖ•ÁèæÊúâÂà∂Â∫¶Ôºå‰πÉÂæ™ÊÑõÂúãÊÉÖÊá∑ÊàñÊ∞ëÊóè‰∏ªÁæ©ÁöÑÊñπÂêëÂ∞ãÊâæË∫´‰ªΩ„ÄÇ

Hong Kong

Historical Period: Pre-1980

        1. Formation of the ‚ÄúPartnership‚Äù Relationship Between the Government and Christianity

          In 1971, the Hong Kong government implemented a six-year compulsory primary education policy to address the educational needs of school-age children. By 1978, this was expanded to cover junior secondary education, establishing nine years of compulsory education (Cheng Kai-ming, 1997: 469-471). To meet the demand for primary and secondary school placements, the government increased the number of subsidized schools, providing funding to school-organizing bodies. Under this policy, many church-affiliated schools became government-subsidized schools. Even the Baptist Church, which traditionally adhered to the principle of separating church and state, began indirectly accepting government funding for educational initiatives. This indirect approach involved allowing the Hong Kong Baptist Convention to organize an independent educational institution, financially separate from the Convention. In its initial stages, the institution received loans from the Convention for operations. According to statistics from the Hong Kong Christian Council, in 1979, churches in Hong Kong (including Catholic and Protestant organizations) operated 220 secondary schools, 270 primary schools, and 194 kindergartens, with students in church-run schools accounting for 44% of the total student population (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 38).

          In 1973, the Hong Kong government released its second White Paper on Social Welfare, titled Social Welfare in Hong Kong: The Way Ahead. Its significance included: (1) the government‚Äôs commitment to assuming the majority of welfare expenditures and (2) the formal introduction of the concept of ‚Äúpartnership‚Äù between the Social Welfare Department and voluntary agencies. This partnership delineated the boundaries of their responsibilities while affirming the principle of government subsidies (Chow Wing-sun, 1980: 36-37). In 1979, the government published another White Paper, Social Welfare into the 1980s, which formalized the model of services provided by voluntary agencies with government funding. This brought most voluntary agencies under the government‚Äôs welfare system, necessitating adjustments to their roles and contributions as the government‚Äôs involvement in welfare increased (Ho Fai-chui, 1980).

          Since Christianity had already been significantly involved in social welfare in Hong Kong, Christian-affiliated welfare organizations officially began receiving government funding. As overseas funding decreased after the 1960s, government grants became the primary source of income for Christian welfare services. These policies indirectly established a ‚Äúpartnership‚Äù between the government and Christianity.

          The educational and social welfare policies of the 1970s led Christian school-organizing bodies and social welfare institutions to accept government funding, thereby solidifying the partnership between the Hong Kong government and Christianity. Scholars later referred to this relationship as ‚Äúinstitutional channeling,‚Äù whereby the government influenced and guided religious organizations through funding subsidies to align with its blueprint (Leung and Chan, 2003).

          ‚Äî

          1. Appointment of Clergy as Unofficial Members of the Legislative Council in 1976

            (Lui Tai-lok, 2020)

            In July 1976, Governor Murray MacLehose appointed Rev. Joyce Bennett (1923‚Äì2015) and Fr. Patrick Terence McGovern (1920‚Äì1984) as unofficial members of the Legislative Council. Both were sworn in October 1976.

            After World War II, Governor Mark Young had proposed reforming the Legislative Council by electing unofficial members, but the plan was ultimately rejected. In 1974, the UK Labour government came to power, and London urged MacLehose to open up the Legislative Council and appoint trade union members. Consequently, MacLehose expanded the Legislative Council to 46 members, increasing the maximum number of official members to 18 and unofficial members to 23, making unofficial members the majority. In selecting new unofficial members, MacLehose demonstrated wisdom by appointing two Christian clergy members from the education and labor sectors.

            Rev. Joyce Bennett, born in the UK, graduated in history from the University of London in 1944 and later earned a teaching diploma. She trained as a missionary at the Church Missionary Society Training College (1947‚Äì48) and came to Hong Kong in 1949, teaching at St. Stephen‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College, later becoming the principal. After pursuing theology in the UK in 1960, she taught at Chung Chi College in 1962 and was ordained as a deaconess by Bishop Ronald Hall that same year. In 1965, she was assigned to St. Barnabas‚Äô Church, Kwun Tong, where she established schools. She founded St. Catherine‚Äôs School for Girls in 1968 and became its principal. On November 28, 1971, she and Lydia Wong were ordained as the first female priests in the global Anglican Communion.

            In her memoir, Bennett recalled initially declining MacLehose‚Äôs invitation, citing her reluctance as a missionary to engage in politics. MacLehose reportedly responded, ‚ÄúThere is no politics in Hong Kong.‚Äù After consulting Bishop John Baker, who supported her decision, she accepted the appointment. As a Legislative Council member, she advocated for the interests of all Hong Kong citizens, especially the poor, and focused on education and youth issues. She also served on various committees, including the Police Complaints Committee and the Curriculum Development Committee. Bennett was awarded the OBE in 1978, retired to the UK in 1983, and received an honorary Doctor of Social Sciences from the University of Hong Kong in 1984.

            Fr. Patrick Terence McGovern, an Irish Jesuit, studied sociology and industrial relations at Loyola University, USA, from 1949‚Äì50. After his ordination in 1953, he worked as a military chaplain in Malaysia (1957‚Äì61) before coming to Hong Kong in 1961. He worked as a student counselor at Wah Yan College and later became the director of Caritas. Following the 1967 riots, McGovern focused on labor issues and founded the Industrial Relations Institute in 1968 to train independent labor leaders and establish unions outside of leftist control. McGovern believed in promoting labor education and fostering harmonious labor relations under just conditions. He was awarded the OBE in 1978 and served as an unofficial member of the Executive Council from 1980‚Äì82. He passed away from a heart attack at Wah Yan College in 1984.

            (1) Opposition to the Ordination of Female Priests in the

            Anglican Church, 1971

            (Zhou Shuyan & Huang Huizhen, 2018: 150-152; Gilbert Baker, 1971)

            In December 1969, Bishop Gilbert Baker initiated discussions on ordaining female priests within the Anglican Diocese of Hong Kong and published a bilingual pastoral letter, The Role of Women Priests, outlining the rationale for ordination. In January 1970, he wrote to Archbishop of Canterbury Arthur M. Ramsey, stating that ‚ÄúHong Kong is one of the most likely places for the ordination of women priests to be realized.‚Äù

            In June 1971, Christian Weekly published an article titled The Christian Church Declares No Women Priests, expressing concerns that ordination could harm relations with the Catholic Church and pose challenges regarding marriage and motherhood. Meanwhile, other articles supported the Anglican Church moving quickly on ordination.

            In 1971, Holy Trinity Church launched a campaign under the name of its ‚Äúmajority congregation,‚Äù circulating a petition titled Opposition to the Ordination of Women Priests in the Anglican Church. It called on parishioners to:

            1. Unite to oppose the ordination of women priests.

              1. Expose the ‚Äúsinister conspiracy‚Äù of those supporting women priests.

                1. Uphold biblical teachings that women should not govern men.

                  1. Protect the dignity of the clergy by ensuring that female deacons do not govern male priests, thereby preserving the honor of the priesthood.

                    1. Support the Anglican Constitution by opposing the ordination of women priests.

                      On October 18, Bishop Baker received a letter from ‚ÄúAll Congregation Members‚Äù urging him to heed Holy Trinity Church‚Äôs opposition, describing female clergy as ‚Äúwicked women clergymen‚Äù and ‚ÄúSatan‚Äôs women.‚Äù On October 20, another letter from ‚ÄúMost Christians Opposing the Appointment of a Woman Priest in Holy Trinity Church‚Äù accused a female deacon, Ms. Wong, of manipulating meetings to achieve her ambition of priesthood. The letter threatened that persisting with ordination would be a ‚Äúgrave sin of betrayal against God‚Äù and bring ‚ÄúCatholic scorn.‚Äù

                      Despite the resistance, Bishop Baker remained resolute. In November 1971, he reiterated his stance in a pastoral letter, declaring, ‚ÄúThe time is now.‚Äù On November 28, 1971, Bishop Baker ordained Lydia Wong and CMS missionary Joyce Bennett, making them the first women priests in the Anglican Communion.

                      ‚Äî

                      (2) Restructuring of the Chinese University of Hong Kong and Its

                      Impact on Christian Traditions

                      When the Chinese University of Hong Kong (CUHK) was founded in 1963, it adopted a federal structure, granting significant autonomy to its founding colleges: New Asia, Chung Chi, and United. Chung Chi College, with its Christian background, retained its traditions in theological education and chapel worship, even as part of a public university. However, by the 1970s, the federal structure became a contentious issue, with concerns over overlapping administrative frameworks.

                      In November 1975, Governor Murray MacLehose appointed an external commission led by Lord John S. Fulton to review the university‚Äôs structure. The Fulton Report, published in March 1976, recommended centralizing authority within the university administration, granting it control over academic policies, financial management, admissions, curriculum development, examinations, and degree conferrals. This marked a pivotal event in CUHK‚Äôs development, as it concentrated administrative and developmental power in the central university.

                      The move toward centralization raised concerns among Christian leaders about its impact on Chung Chi‚Äôs identity. Rev. Lau Chi-ping questioned, ‚ÄúIf centralization is adopted, will Chung Chi College remain a Christian university?‚Äù Critics feared the marginalization of theological education and chapel roles. Bishop Baker of the Anglican Church publicly expressed concerns about the reforms‚Äô effect on Chung Chi‚Äôs Christian traditions. The college‚Äôs board of trustees also held reservations, and Chung Chi President Yung Kai-tong criticized the reforms as direct government interference in CUHK‚Äôs internal affairs.

                      Amid the controversy, the government accepted the Fulton Report‚Äôs recommendations, and on December 22, 1976, the Legislative Council passed new statutes for CUHK. In response, nine trustees of New Asia College, including Ch‚Äôien Mu and Tang Chun-i, collectively resigned. Chung Chi‚Äôs new president, Tam Sheung-wai, issued an open letter assuring the public that the new statutes preserved Chung Chi‚Äôs Christian traditions and theological education.

                      ‚Äî

                      (3) First Colloquium of Six Religious Leaders of Hong Kong,

                      1978

                      On June 16, 1978, representatives from Confucianism, Buddhism, Taoism, Christianity, Catholicism, and Islam held the first Colloquium of Six Religious Leaders of Hong Kong at the Hong Kong Convention Center. The meeting aimed to foster dialogue, unity, and cooperation among religious groups to contribute to Hong Kong society. The theme of the colloquium was media ethics and youth issues. The leaders agreed to strengthen moral education and decided to hold two colloquia annually.

                      Each Lunar New Year, the six religious leaders issued a joint statement. In 1979, their New Year message focused on supporting the International Year of the Child.

                      1. The Transformations of China in the 1970s and Hong Kong

                      (Carroll 2007: 176‚Äì179; Lui Tai-lok 2020: 150‚Äì154)

                      As Hong Kong entered the 1970s, the shocks of two prior riots forced the colonial government to accelerate its pace of reform. The 1971 population census revealed a demographic continuation of the trend noted in 1966, with over half of the population being locally born, ushering in a new era for the decade. The locally born younger generation abandoned the refugee mentality of their predecessors. While they developed a stronger sense of identification with and expectations for Hong Kong society, they could not fully escape the profound changes taking place in China during the 1970s. A distinct local society was gradually forming, yet it remained inseparable from its connection with China.

                      Undoubtedly, changes in Sino-U.S. relations were a crucial factor behind the dramatic transformations in China during the 1970s. In 1971, China invited the U.S. table tennis team for a visit, an act widely interpreted as an attempt to break the ice in Sino-U.S. relations through ‚Äúping-pong diplomacy.‚Äù In July, U.S. Secretary of State Henry Kissinger made a secret visit to Beijing, during which he pledged U.S. support for the People‚Äôs Republic of China to gain membership in the United Nations and a seat on the Security Council during the upcoming General Assembly session. Kissinger also agreed on a visit by President Richard Nixon to China before May 1972.

                      After Kissinger‚Äôs visit, the United Nations General Assembly passed Resolution 2758 on October 25, which restored the People‚Äôs Republic of China‚Äôs legitimate rights within the United Nations. The following day, Chiang Kai-shek issued a statement announcing the Republic of China‚Äôs withdrawal from the United Nations. This vote was considered a significant diplomatic victory for the People‚Äôs Republic of China, with U.S. support playing a pivotal role.

                      In February 1972, at the invitation of Premier Zhou Enlai, President Nixon visited China, becoming the first U.S. president to do so in history. Following the visit, China and the U.S. issued the Shanghai Communiqu√©, marking a gradual normalization of relations after over two decades of hostility.

                      However, the changes in Sino-U.S. relations were part of a broader triangular contest among China, the U.S., and the Soviet Union. For China, which was still engulfed in the fervor of the Cultural Revolution, these developments seemed to bring little immediate change. It wasn‚Äôt until Mao Zedong‚Äôs declining health in 1976 that these shifts could be seen as precursors to major transformations. The subsequent arrests of the Gang of Four, Deng Xiaoping‚Äôs return to power, and China‚Äôs emergence from the shadows of the Cultural Revolution in 1979 marked the nation‚Äôs formal farewell to revolutionary politics and the beginning of a new era of economic reform and opening up. That same year, China and the U.S. formally established diplomatic relations, with Deng Xiaoping visiting the U.S. shortly afterward.

                      What impact did the transformations in China during the 1970s have on Hong Kong?

                      Firstly, following China‚Äôs entry into the United Nations, its UN representative Huang Hua declared in March 1972 that Hong Kong‚Äôs political status was ‚ÄúChinese territory occupied by British authorities‚Äù and demanded the removal of Hong Kong and Macau from the UN list of colonies. Huang emphasized that the Hong Kong issue fell within China‚Äôs sovereignty and that the Chinese government would resolve it ‚Äúat an appropriate time, in an appropriate manner.‚Äù On November 2, 1972, the United Nations General Assembly passed Resolution 2908 with 99 votes in favor and 5 against, officially removing Hong Kong and Macau from the list of colonies. The United Kingdom did not object to this resolution.

                      The most significant implication of this move was the deprivation of the right to self-determination, previously granted under the UN‚Äôs 1960 Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples.

                      In response to these changes and to align with the demands of both the British and Chinese governments, Hong Kong‚Äôs status shifted from a Crown Colony to a Dependent Territory. However, the term ‚Äúcolony‚Äù remained on its banknotes until 1985. Furthermore, the title of Colonial Secretary was changed to Chief Secretary, and the United Kingdom ceased its annual reports on Hong Kong to the UN Secretary-General.

                      What did Huang Hua mean by resolving the Hong Kong issue ‚Äúat an appropriate time, in an appropriate manner‚Äù? At the time, no one could provide a definitive answer. However, when Portugal proposed returning Macau in 1974, and the Chinese government declined, it sparked speculation that China might also be reluctant to reclaim Hong Kong.

                      Nonetheless, with the lease on the New Territories set to expire in 1997, the Hong Kong government could no longer issue land leases extending beyond that date. The year 1997 undoubtedly became a ‚Äúdeadline‚Äù for Hong Kong‚Äôs future. Murray MacLehose, who became Governor of Hong Kong in November 1971, was aware of this issue even before assuming office. He summarized London‚Äôs stance on Hong Kong as follows:

  1. avoid direct confrontation with the Chinese government and refrain from initiating negotiations on Hong Kong‚Äôs future in the short term;
  2. design policies that could sustain public confidence in the government, thereby buying sufficient time to wait for favorable conditions for negotiations with China. He emphasized, ‚ÄúThe critical moment will not begin until the late 1970s, but the planning process must start now.‚Äù

    In January 1973, MacLehose stated in his 1972 annual report that ‚Äúsooner or later, we must discuss with the Chinese government what will happen when the lease on the New Territories expires in 1997.‚Äù He firmly believed that before entering negotiations, the Hong Kong government needed to ‚Äúput its house in order,‚Äù meaning to develop Hong Kong to be prosperous, cohesive, and content, so that it would not become a target of criticism. He also stressed that if the Chinese government considered Hong Kong‚Äôs economic benefits, ‚Äúit might be unwilling to reclaim Hong Kong‚Äù and might even consider granting Hong Kong special status under its sovereignty. In sum, Hong Kong needed to become ‚Äúa model city.‚Äù However, MacLehose cautioned that this should be pursued discreetly, with minimal rhetoric and without overtly challenging China.

    In May 1974, Edward Heath, former British Prime Minister and leader of the Conservative Party, visited Beijing. During a banquet hosted by Vice Premier Deng Xiaoping, Deng stated that the Hong Kong issue would be resolved at an appropriate time. In March 1979, the Chinese government invited MacLehose to visit Beijing, marking the first official visit by a sitting Hong Kong Governor to China. MacLehose viewed this as an opportunity to discuss the lease of the New Territories. During his meeting with Deng Xiaoping, MacLehose proposed that the Legislative Council could unilaterally disregard the 1997 deadline without asserting sovereignty over New Territories land. This suggestion was rejected by Deng. It is widely believed that during this meeting, Deng expressed China‚Äôs intention to reclaim Hong Kong by 1997. However, upon returning to Hong Kong, MacLehose only stated that Deng was committed to resolving the Hong Kong issue eventually and reassured Hong Kong investors to ‚Äúrest assured.‚Äù

    In 1981, the United Kingdom passed the British Nationality Act, which downgraded the status of 2.5 million Hong Kong residents eligible for British passports to British Dependent Territories Citizens (BDTC), effectively stripping them of the right to reside in the United Kingdom. This move reflected Britain‚Äôs preparations for 1997 and closed the door on Hong Kong residents migrating to Britain.

    ÁÇ∫ÂèçÊò†ÈÄôÁ®ÆËÆäÂåñÔºå‰∏¶‰πéÂêàËã±‰∏≠ÂÖ©ÂúãÊîøÂ∫úÁöÑË¶ÅÊ±ÇÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁî±Áõ¥ËΩÑÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞ÔºàCrown ColonyÔºâËÆäÊàêÂ±¨Âú∞ÔºàDependent TerritoryÔºâÔºå‰ΩÜ„ÄåÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞„Äç‰∏ÄË©û‰ªçÂç∞Âú®ÈàîÁ•®‰∏äÔºàÁõ¥Ëá≥1985Âπ¥Ôºâ„ÄÇËÄåËã±ÊñáÂéüÁ®±Colonial SecretaryÁöÑËºîÊîøÂè∏Ôºå‰πüÊîπÂêçChief SecretaryÔºàÂ∏ÉÊîøÂè∏Ôºâ„ÄÇËã±Âúã‰πü‰∏çÁî®ÊØèÂπ¥ÂêëËÅØÂêàÂúãÁßòÊõ∏Èï∑ÂëàËøëÊúâÈóúÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÂ†±Âëä„ÄÇ

    1973Âπ¥1ÊúàÔºåÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Êèê‰∫§ÁöÑ1972Âπ¥ÈÄ±Âπ¥Â†±Âëä‰∏≠ÊåáÂá∫Ôºö„ÄåÊàñÈÅ≤ÊàñÊó©ÔºåÊàëÂÄëÂøÖÈ†àË∑ü‰∏≠Âúã‰∫∫Ê∞ëÊîøÂ∫úË®éË´ñÂà∞1997Âπ¥ÁßüÁ¥ÑÊúüÊªøÊâÄÁôºÁîüÁöÑ‰∫ãÊÉÖ„Äç„ÄÇ‰ªñÊ∑±‰ø°ÔºåÂú®Ë´áÂà§ÂâçÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊîøÂ∫úÈúÄË¶Å„ÄåÊääÂÖßÂãôÁÆ°ÁêÜÂæóÂ¶•Â¶•Áï∂Áï∂Ôºàto put its house in orderÔºâ„ÄçÔºåÂç≥„ÄåÁõ°ÈáèÁôºÂ±ïÂæóÁπÅÊ¶Æ„ÄÅÂúòÁµê„ÄÅÔºàÊ∞ëÁúæÔºâÊªøË∂≥Ôºå‰ª•Ëá≥‰∏çÊúÉÊàêÁÇ∫ÊâπË©ïÁöÑË©±ÊüÑ„Äç„ÄÇ‰ªñÂèàÂº∑Ë™øÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØËÉΩÁÇ∫‰∏≠ÂúãÊèê‰æõÁöÑÁ∂ìÊøüÂäüËÉΩËàáÂà©ÁõäÔºåÂ¶ÇÊûú‰∏≠ÂúãÊîøÂ∫úËÄÉÊÖÆÂà∞ÈÄô‰∫õÂØ¶Ë≥™Âà©ÁõäÁöÑÈúÄË¶ÅÔºå„ÄåËÄå‰∏çÈ°òÊÑèÊääÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊî∂Âõû„ÄçÔºåÁîöËá≥ËÄÉÊÖÆ„ÄåÂú®ÂÆÉÁöÑ‰∏ªÊ¨ä‰πã‰∏ãÁπºÁ∫åÁµ¶‰∫àÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑ‰∏ÄÂÄãÁâπÊÆäÂú∞‰Ωç„Äç„ÄÇÁ∏Ω‰πãÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØË¶Å„ÄåÊàêÁÇ∫‰∏ÄÊ®°ÁØÑÂüéÂ∏ÇÔºàa model city Ôºâ„ÄÇ‰ΩÜÊàëÂÄëÊáâË©≤‰ΩéË™øÈÄ≤Ë°åÔºåÂ∞ëË™™Ë©±Ôºå‰∏çÂ∞ç‰∏≠Âúã‰ΩúÂá∫Ë°®Èù¢ÁöÑÊåëÊà∞„Äç„ÄÇ

    1974Âπ¥5ÊúàÔºåÂâõËæ≠‰ªªÈ¶ñÁõ∏ÁöÑËã±Âúã‰øùÂÆàÈª®Èª®È≠ÅÂ∏åÊÄùÔºàEdward Richard George HeathÔºâË®™ÂïèÂåó‰∫¨ÔºåÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥ÂâØÁ∏ΩÁêÜÂú®Ê≠°ËøéÂ∏åÊÄùÁöÑÂÆ¥ÊúÉ‰∏äË°®Á§∫ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂïèÈ°åÂ∞áÂú®ÈÅ©Áï∂ÊôÇÂÄôËß£Ê±∫„ÄÇ1979Âπ¥3ÊúàÔºå‰∏≠ÂúãÊîøÂ∫úÈÇÄË´ãÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Ë®™ÂïèÂåó‰∫¨ÔºåÊòØÈ¶ñÊ¨°Âú®‰ªªÊ∏ØÁù£ÂÆòÂºèË®™Âïè‰∏≠ÂúãÔºåÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Ë¶ñÁÇ∫Ë®éË´ñÊñ∞ÁïåÁßüÁ¥ÑÂïèÈ°åÁöÑÊ©üÊúÉ„ÄÇ‰ªñÂú®Âåó‰∫¨‰∏≠ÂúãÊñ∞È†òÂ∞é‰∫∫ÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥ÊúÉÈù¢ÊôÇÔºåÂª∫Ë≠∞Á´ãÊ≥ïÂ±ÄÂú®‰∏çÊèêÂá∫ÊìÅÊúâÊñ∞ÁïåÂúüÂú∞‰∏ªÊ¨äÁöÑÊÉÖÊ≥Å‰∏ãÔºåÂèØ‰ª•ÂñÆÊñπÈù¢‰∏çÁêÜÊúÉ1997Âπ¥ÁöÑÊúüÈôê„ÄÇÊ≠§Âª∫Ë≠∞Ë¢´ÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥ÊãíÁµï„ÄÇ‰∏ÄËà¨Áõ∏‰ø°ÔºåÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥Âú®Ê≠§Ê¨°ÊúÉË´á‰∏≠Ë°®Á§∫Â∞áÊñº1997Âπ¥Êî∂ÂõûÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ‰ΩÜÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©ÂõûÂà∞È¶ôÊ∏ØÊôÇÔºåÂè™Ë°®Á§∫ÈÑßÂ∞èÂπ≥‰∏ªÂºµ‰∏≠ÂúãÊúÄÁµÇÊúÉËß£Ê±∫È¶ôÊ∏ØÂïèÈ°åÔºå‰∏¶Ë°®Á§∫È¶ôÊ∏ØÊäïË≥áËÄÖÂèØ‰ª•„ÄéÊîæÂøÉ„Äè„Äç„ÄÇ

    (2) The Transformation of Hong Kong in the 1970s

    (Carroll 2007: 168‚Äì176; Lui Tai-lok 2020: 141‚Äì186; Yep 2024)

    The 1970s are often referred to as the ‚ÄúMacLehose Era.‚Äù Sir Murray MacLehose served as the Governor of Hong Kong from November 1971 until May 1982, with his tenure extended four times between 1977 and 1979, making him the longest-serving governor in Hong Kong‚Äôs colonial history. MacLehose‚Äôs policies and reforms were, on the one hand, a continuation of the principles laid out by his predecessor, David Trench, which sought to address the riots of 1966 and 1967 through reform. On the other hand, they were intended to address the question of Hong Kong‚Äôs future, aiming to transform Hong Kong into a ‚Äúmodel city‚Äù that would secure favorable terms in future Sino-British negotiations.

    During MacLehose‚Äôs tenure, significant advancements were made in various areas: public housing (the Ten-Year Housing Program in 1973 and the Home Ownership Scheme in 1976), nine years of free compulsory education (1978), public assistance (allowances for the disabled and elderly in 1973 and the Social Security Green Paper in 1977), transportation (the proposal to build the Mass Transit Railway in 1972), labor legislation (the Severance Payment Ordinance in 1974, amendments to the Labour Tribunal Ordinance in 1974, and seven days of paid annual leave in 1977), and social welfare (the White Papers on Social Welfare in 1973 and 1979).

    In governance, all ex officio members of the Urban Council were removed in 1973, leaving only appointed and elected members. Although no significant administrative or legislative council reforms occurred, the number of Legislative Council seats was increased in 1976, and several grassroots-oriented unofficial members were appointed, including Reverend Joyce Bennett (1923‚Äì2015) and Father Patrick Terence McGovern (1920‚Äì1984).

    Other initiatives included the Clean Hong Kong Campaign (1972), the Fight Violent Crime Campaign (1973), the Country Parks Program (1976), and the legalization of Chinese as an official language (1974). A major achievement was the establishment of the Independent Commission Against Corruption (ICAC) in 1974 to combat entrenched corruption.

    The extensive reforms led to a substantial increase in government expenditure, which rose by 50% between 1970 and 1972 and continued to grow thereafter. The 1975 budget was the largest postwar budget in Hong Kong‚Äôs history, with a deficit of 900 million Hong Kong dollars and the introduction of 26 new taxes across four categories. There is no doubt that the MacLehose era marked a departure from laissez-faire economic policies toward the establishment of a welfare state.

    Overall, the MacLehose era was a period of robust economic growth in Hong Kong, with a consistent rise in GDP. Hong Kong became a global leader in the export value and volume of garments, toys, plastics, and watches, establishing itself as one of Asia‚Äôs leading light industrial hubs. The government‚Äôs implementation of the Home Ownership Scheme also led to a surge in private residential construction, which further spurred the real estate sector and contributed to economic growth.

    However, the period also saw challenges. The 1973 oil crisis triggered by the Middle East war caused a stock market crash in July, with the Hang Seng Index plunging by 91.54% (from a high of 1,174.96 in March 1973 to 150.11 on December 10, 1974). Between 1972 and 1975, Hong Kong experienced an economic downturn. Trade protectionist measures by various countries caused a 7% decline in real export volumes in 1974, leading to factory closures and widespread underemployment. By 1975, unemployment had risen to 200,000, with 120,000 in the industrial sector and 80,000 in commerce.

    By 1976, the economy began to recover, and in 1979, China‚Äôs economic reforms provided new opportunities for growth in Hong Kong.

    Áî±ÊñºÂêÑÈ†ÖÊîπÈù©ÔºåÊîøÂ∫úÁöÑÈñãÊîØÂ§ßÂπÖÂ¢ûÂä†Ôºå1970Âπ¥Ëá≥72Âπ¥ÈñìÔºåÂ¢ûÂπÖÈÅî50%ÔºåÊ≠§ÂæåÁπºÁ∫å‰∏äÂçá„ÄÇ1975Âπ¥ÁöÑË≤°ÊîøÈ†êÁÆóÊ°àÔºåÊõ¥ÊòØÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊà∞Âæå‰ª•‰æÜÊúÄÈæêÂ§ßÁöÑÂä†Á®ÖÂä†Ë≤ªÈ†êÁÆóÊ°àÔºåËµ§Â≠óÈÅî9ÂÑÑÊ∏ØÂÖÉÔºåÂêåÊôÇÂÆ£‰ΩàÂØ¶Ë°å4Â§ßÈ°û26È†ÖÂä†Á®ÖÊé™ÊñΩ„ÄÇÊØ´ÁÑ°ÁñëÂïèÔºåÈ∫•ÁêÜÊµ©ÊôÇ‰ª£ÊîπËÆä‰∫Ü„ÄåÊîæ‰ªªËá™ÊµÅ„ÄçÁöÑÁ∂ìÊøüÊîøÁ≠ñÔºåÊúùÂêëÂª∫Á´ã‰∏ÄÂÄãÁ¶èÂà©Á§æÊúÉ„ÄÇ

    1. Nationalism and Hong Kong Identity

    (Lui Tai-lok 2020: 188‚Äì207; Law Wing-sang 2015; Law Wing-sang 2017: 76‚Äì80)

    It is widely believed that after the two riots in the 1960s, the colonial government deliberately fostered and strengthened a sense of belonging among Hong Kong citizens. Coupled with the Cultural Revolution and the 1967 riots, which led to resistance against Communist China, the sense of a ‚ÄúHong Konger‚Äù identity began to gradually take shape.

    However, in the early 1970s, there was still a strong sense of curiosity among young university students in Hong Kong to learn about their ‚Äúmotherland.‚Äù Specifically, this was reflected in the Chinese Language Movement (1968‚Äì1971) and the Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement (1970‚Äì1972). Both were student-led social movements with strong nationalist orientations.

    The Chinese Language Movement aimed to make Chinese an official language in Hong Kong, challenging the colonial policy of long-standing official preference for English. Advocates argued that most Hong Kong residents spoke Chinese as their mother tongue and sought equal treatment for the language. By 1970, various academic groups had mobilized to gain broader social attention for the movement. Some voices even suggested that the movement should go beyond nationalism to incorporate anti-colonialism. In September 1970, the government began studying the use of Chinese in official documents. In November, the Legislative Council amended its rules to allow council members to speak in English or Cantonese. In October 1971, simultaneous interpretation between English and Cantonese was introduced for the first time. Finally, in February 1974, the Legislative Council passed a third reading, formally designating Chinese as an official language and allowing Chinese dialects to be used in courts.

    The Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement arose in response to the U.S. decision to transfer the Diaoyu Islands and its affiliated islets to Japan. This incited protests from Hong Kong university students. In July 1971, the Hong Kong Federation of Students held a demonstration in Victoria Park. Riot police were deployed, resulting in clashes and arrests. In August and September, the police permitted further demonstrations in Victoria Park, which attracted nearly a thousand participants. The Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement not only heightened the nationalist sentiment among Hong Kong students but also evolved into a patriotic movement aligned with Communist China as students sought a sense of belonging through nationalist fervor. This identification with ‚Äúthe motherland‚Äù reflected the inability of young students, under colonial circumstances, to fully integrate into the existing system. Instead, they sought identity through nationalism and patriotic sentiment.

    Both the Chinese Language Movement and the Diaoyu Islands Defense Movement exemplified the fervent atmosphere of student activism in Hong Kong during the ‚Äúfiery years‚Äù of the 1970s. In December 1971, the University of Hong Kong Students‚Äô Union organized the first ‚ÄúHomeland Tour,‚Äù which was warmly received by mainland officials. This ignited a wave of interest among university students to organize study tours to China. During this period, campus ideology leaned toward leftist socialism, with the ‚ÄúCultural Purists‚Äù dominating the student movement. Student unions organized ‚ÄúChina Weeks,‚Äù which focused on showcasing the achievements of the Communist Party, praising Mao Zedong, and celebrating the Cultural Revolution. China‚Äôs rising international status‚Äîgaining a seat in the United Nations and the global ‚ÄúChina fever‚Äù in the West‚Äîfurther reinforced the beliefs of the Cultural Purists. In March 1975, they introduced the slogan: ‚ÄúLook at the world, understand the motherland, care about society, and fight for student rights,‚Äù reaffirming Hong Kong‚Äôs inseparability from China.

    In contrast to the Cultural Purists, there was another faction within university campuses known as the Social Activists. While also aligned with leftist ideologies, the Social Activists believed that the student movement should address the issues facing Hong Kong society and pursue social reform through ‚Äúanti-capitalism and anti-colonialism.‚Äù They rejected the unconditional alignment with Communist China advocated by the Cultural Purists, placing greater emphasis on social engagement rather than emotional nationalism as a means to understand China.

    After 1976, with the death of Mao Zedong, the end of the Cultural Revolution, and the downfall of the Gang of Four, the Cultural Purists gradually declined. Consequently, the Hong Kong student movement shifted toward the Social Activists‚Äô approach, focusing on colonial social issues. By the mid-1970s, the general public in Hong Kong had also become more concerned with various social problems, leading to the emergence of social struggles centered on protecting rights and interests.

    Simultaneously, the reforms during the MacLehose era addressed some of the public‚Äôs expectations of the colonial government. Hong Kong society began to develop a localized political agenda, forming a ‚Äúself-contained‚Äù local society that moved beyond the ideological struggles between the Chinese Nationalists and Communists or the broader left-right divide.

    By 1978, Hong Kong‚Äôs population had reached 4.5 million. However, it also faced a surge of illegal immigrants from China. As early as November 1974, the Hong Kong government had reached a verbal agreement with Guangdong authorities allowing the repatriation of captured Chinese illegal immigrants to the mainland (though immigrants who successfully entered urban areas would be permitted to stay). In 1979, Xi Zhongxun, Director of the Guangdong Revolutionary Committee, stated that the Chinese government was taking ‚Äúeffective measures‚Äù to address the ‚Äúabnormal situation‚Äù of legal and illegal immigration from China to Hong Kong.

    Faced with the influx of ‚Äúnew immigrants‚Äù from China, Hong Kong residents developed a ‚Äúself-protective‚Äù mindset, consciously defining who belonged and who did not. Concerns arose over whether the ‚Äúnew immigrants‚Äù would place a burden on Hong Kong and impact the rights of local residents.
